<span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F811%2F1%2F20\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/811/1/20</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n            <P>Ongoing and future imaging surveys represent significant improvements in depth, area, and seeing compared to current data sets. These improvements offer the opportunity to discover up to three orders of magnitude more galaxy\u2013galaxy strong lenses than are currently known. In this work we forecast the number of lenses that will be discoverable in forthcoming surveys and simulate their properties. We generate a population of statistically realistic strong lenses and simulate observations of this population for the Dark Energy Survey (DES), the Large Synoptic Survey Telescope (LSST), and Euclid surveys. We verify our model against the galaxy-scale lens search of the Canada\u2013France\u2013Hawaii Telescope Legacy Survey, predicting 250 discoverable lenses compared to 220 found by Gavazzi et al. The predicted Einstein radius distribution is also remarkably similar to that found by Sonnenfeld et al. For future surveys we find that, assuming Poisson limited lens galaxy subtraction, searches of the DES, LSST, and Euclid data sets should discover 2400, 120000, and 170000 galaxy\u2013galaxy strong lenses, respectively. Finders using blue-minus-red (<IMG ALIGN=\"MIDDLE\" alt=\"$g-i$\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/apj519046ieqn1.gif\"></IMG>) difference imaging for lens subtraction can discover 1300 and 62000 lenses in DES and LSST. The uncertainties on the model are dominated by the high-redshift source population, which typically gives fractional errors on the discoverable lens number at the level of tens of percent. We find that doubling the signal-to-noise ratio required for a lens to be detectable approximately halves the number of detectable lenses in each survey, indicating the importance of understanding the selection function and the sensitivity of future lens finders in interpreting strong lens statistics. We make our population forecasting and simulated observation codes publicly available so that the selection function of strong lens finders can easily be calibrated.</P></div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/811/1/20&doi=10.1088/0004-637X/811/1/20&exportFormat=iopexport_bib&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-bib\" title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/811/1/20&doi=10.1088/0004-637X/811/1/20&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<!-- Start AAS Related Links -->\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2015ApJ...811...20C\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/0004-637X/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj519046s1\" name=\"apj519046s1\">1.\u00a0INTRODUCTION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Strong gravitational lensing by galaxies can be used to probe both astrophysics and cosmology. To date several hundred galaxy\u2013galaxy strong lenses have been discovered in heterogeneous searches of photometric and spectroscopic survey data (Myers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib43\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib43\">2003</a>; Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib5\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib5\">2006</a>; Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib22\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib22\">2012</a>; Inada et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib26\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib26\">2012</a>; Negrello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib44\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib44\">2014</a>). The sample has been used to constrain the masses and density profiles of galaxies (Auger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib1\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib1\">2010</a>; Barnab\u00e8 et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib2\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib2\">2011</a>), the dark sub-halo population (Vegetti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib66\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib66\">2014</a>), cosmological parameters (Oguri et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib46\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib46\">2012</a>; Collett &amp; Auger <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib10\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib10\">2014</a>; Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib64\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib64\">2014</a>), the high-redshift luminosity function (Barone-Nugent et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib3\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib3\">2015</a>), and the nature of high-redshift sources (e.g., Quider et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib52\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib52\">2009</a>; Newton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib45\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib45\">2011</a>). For many of these analyses the shortage of suitable strong lenses is a major limiting factor.</p><p>Several ongoing and near-future wide and deep sky surveys offer improved depth, area, and resolution compared to existing data (Miyazaki et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib39\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib39\">2006</a>; Ivezic et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib27\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib27\">2008</a>; Laureijs et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib33\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib33\">2011</a>; Diehl et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib16\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib16\">2014</a>). These surveys have the potential to increase the current galaxy-scale lens sample by orders of magnitude (Kuhlen et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib31\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib31\">2004</a>; Marshall et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib35\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib35\">2005a</a>). With a large increase to the known strong lens population, current work could be extended to new regimes; lower lens masses, higher-redshift lenses, and intrinsically fainter sources. In turn this will allow investigations into the luminosity and redshift trends of lens and source properties. For example, investigating trends in the mass-to-light ratio of galaxies, the distribution of dark matter in galaxies and the dark-substructure population will much more tightly constrain the nature of dark matter, the initial mass function, and galaxy formation physics than is currently possible (e.g., Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib59\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib59\">2013a</a>; Vegetti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib66\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib66\">2014</a>).</p><p>Future surveys also have the potential to discover a population of exotic\u2014and rare\u2014strongly lensed systems. For example, compound lenses are powerful probes of dark matter (Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib60\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib60\">2012</a>) and cosmological parameters (Collett et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib11\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib11\">2012</a>), as are strongly lensed supernovae (Refsdal <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib54\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib54\">1964</a>) and strong lensing catastrophes (Orban de Xivry &amp; Marshall <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib48\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib48\">2009</a>), but only a handful of these systems are known (Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib21\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib21\">2008</a>; Quimby et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib53\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib53\">2014</a>; Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib29\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib29\">2015</a>), which limits the precision of cosmological constraints achievable with strong lensing (Collett &amp; Auger <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib10\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib10\">2014</a>).</p><p>In addition to science using sub-samples of lenses, the size and properties of the full ensemble of strong lenses is dependent on both cosmological parameters and the source and deflector populations (e.g., Oguri et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib46\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib46\">2012</a>); future surveys will provide a much more powerful sample for strong lensing statistics.</p><p>Recent analytical work has forecast the number of lensed point sources likely to be discovered in spectroscopic surveys (Serjeant <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib56\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib56\">2014</a>) and in photometric surveys (Oguri &amp; Marshall <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib47\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib47\">2010</a>); however, making forecasts for lenses with extended background sources requires one to move beyond analytic analyses, due to differential magnification across the source plane. Dobler et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib18\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib18\">2008</a>) simulated lensing of spectral line emission regions and then simulated observing such objects with the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS) spectrograph to model the selection function in the SLACS survey. By changing the source population to galaxies (Ilbert et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib25\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib25\">2009</a>) and the transfer function to that of forthcoming surveys (e.g., Chang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib8\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib8\">2015</a>) the methodology of Dobler et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib18\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib18\">2008</a>) can be extended to predict galaxy\u2013galaxy strong lensing rates in forthcoming photometric surveys.</p><p>The goal of this work is to answer the question <em>\"How many galaxy\u2013galaxy lenses can forthcoming imaging surveys potentially discover?\"</em> To answer this question we first build a population of realistic strong lenses (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj519046s2\">2</a>) and then simulate observations of these lenses (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj519046s3\">3</a>) for four recent and forthcoming photometric surveys; the Canada\u2013France\u2013Hawaii Telescope Legacy Survey (CFHTLS), the Dark Energy Survey (DES), the Large Synoptic Survey Telescope (LSST), and Euclid. Of course, actually discovering strong lenses in these surveys will require the development of new methods and algorithms (e.g., Brault &amp; Gavazzi <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib7\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib7\">2014</a>; Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib20\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib20\">2014</a>; K\u00fcng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib32\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib32\">2015</a>), which is beyond the scope of this article, but this work is intended to motivate such developments by providing both realistic expectations for lens finders and realistic simulations with which to test lens finders. We also investigate how the features of lens finders can affect the number of strong lenses that they can discover, paying attention to how they alter the effective seeing and signal-to-noise of the data. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj519046s7\">7</a> we discuss our results in the context of the uncertainties inherent to how we have constructed the lens population and how we have assumed future lens finders might work. We summarize and conclude our results in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj519046s8\">8</a>. Throughout this paper we assume a flat \u039bCDM cosmology, with <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn2.gif\" alt=\"${{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{M}=0.7$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{M}=0.7</script></span></span>, and <em>h</em> = 0.7.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj519046s2\" name=\"apj519046s2\">2.\u00a0CREATING A POPULATION OF REALISTIC MOCK GALAXY\u2013GALAXY STRONG LENSES</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Strong gravitational lensing occurs when a massive foreground object is sufficiently well aligned with a background source that the lens equation has multiple solutions and hence multiple images of the source can form. The population of strong lenses therefore depends on the population of massive objects, background sources, and the geometry of the universe. Once the lens population is known, individual strong lens systems can be drawn from the population and simulated to assess if strong lensing is detectable. Simulating individual systems requires knowledge of the light profile of the lens and source, the density profile of the source and the angular diameter distances between observer, lens, and source.</p><h3 id=\"apj519046s2-1\" name=\"apj519046s2-1\">2.1.\u00a0The Foreground Deflector Population</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Observations have shown that the mass profiles of elliptical galaxies are well-approximated by isothermal mass distributions (e.g., Auger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib1\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib1\">2010</a>). In this work we will consider only strong lensing by elliptical galaxies, which dominate the galaxy\u2013galaxy lensing cross section (Oguri &amp; Marshall <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib47\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib47\">2010</a>), and then assume that all of the foreground deflectors are singular isothermal ellipsoids (SIEs). The density profile of an SIE is given by</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj519046eqn1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046eqn1.gif\" alt=\"Equation (1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&\\rho (\\tilde{r})=\\displaystyle \\frac{{\\sigma }_{V}{}^{2}}{2\\pi G{\\tilde{r}}^{2}}.\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn3.gif\" alt=\"${\\sigma }_{V}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\sigma }_{V}</script></span></span> is the velocity dispersion of the lens, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn4.gif\" alt=\"$\\tilde{r}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\tilde{r}</script></span></span> is the elliptical distance from the center (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn5.gif\" alt=\"${\\tilde{r}}^{2}={x}^{2}/q+{{qy}}^{2}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\tilde{r}}^{2}={x}^{2}/q+{{qy}}^{2}</script></span></span>), and <em>q</em> is the ratio of semi-major and semi-minor axes (i.e., the flattening). Einstein radii of SIEs are given by the stellar velocity dispersion of the galaxy, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn6.gif\" alt=\"${\\sigma }_{V}^{2}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\sigma }_{V}^{2}</script></span></span>, and angular diameter distances between observer, lens, and source (<em>D</em><sub><em>ij</em></sub>)</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj519046eqn2\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046eqn2.gif\" alt=\"Equation (2)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}^{{\\rm{SIS}}}=4\\pi \\displaystyle \\frac{{\\sigma }_{V}^{2}}{{c}^{2}}\\displaystyle \\frac{{D}_{{\\rm{ls}}}}{{D}_{{\\rm{s}}}}.\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>We neglect the lensing contribution of matter along the line of sight.</p><p>For the light profile of the deflector, we assume an elliptical de Vaucolours profile that is concentric with and aligned with the mass (e.g., as observed by Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib21\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib21\">2008</a>; Kostrzewa-Rutkowska et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib30\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib30\">2014</a>). We make the approximation that the effective radius, <em>R</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>, of the deflector is the same in all observed bands, but allow the absolute magnitude <em>M</em> in each band to vary. For the colors of the lens we assume the rest-frame SED of a passive galaxy whose star formation was a single burst 10 Gyrs ago.</p><p>Given these simplifying assumptions, our model for the deflector is thus described by five parameters; the lensredshift, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn7.gif\" alt=\"${\\sigma }_{V}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\sigma }_{V}</script></span></span>, <em>q</em>, <em>R</em><sub>e</sub>, and <em>M</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>. Rotational and translational symmetry allow us to place the deflector at the center of the coordinate system and align the semimajor and coordinate <em>x</em>-axes.</p><p>Choi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib9\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib9\">2007</a>) used SDSS (York et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib71\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib71\">2000</a>) data to derive the velocity dispersion function of elliptical galaxies in the local universe, finding</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj519046eqn3\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046eqn3.gif\" alt=\"Equation (3)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{dn}={\\phi }_{*}{(\\displaystyle \\frac{\\sigma }{{\\sigma }_{*}})}^{\\alpha }\\mathrm{exp}[-{(\\displaystyle \\frac{\\sigma }{{\\sigma }_{*}})}^{\\beta }]\\displaystyle \\frac{\\beta }{{\\rm{\\Gamma }}(\\alpha /\\beta )}\\displaystyle \\frac{d\\sigma }{\\sigma },\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn8.gif\" alt=\"${\\phi }_{*}=8.0\\times {10}^{-3}{h}^{3}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\phi }_{*}=8.0\\times {10}^{-3}{h}^{3}</script></span></span> Mpc<sup>\u22123</sup>, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn9.gif\" alt=\"${\\sigma }_{*}=161$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\sigma }_{*}=161</script></span></span> km s<sup>\u22121</sup>, <em>\u03b1</em> = 2.32, and <em>\u03b2</em> = 2.67. In our model we assume that neither the shape nor the normalization of this function vary with redshift; this is consistent with the observations of Bezanson et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib4\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib4\">2011</a>).</p><p>From Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apj519046eqn3\">3</a>) we can draw a velocity dispersion for each deflector to be simulated and a redshift from the differential comoving volume function <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn10.gif\" alt=\"$d{\\rm{Vol}}/{dz}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">d{\\rm{Vol}}/{dz}</script></span></span>. The other parameters of our lens model, <em>q</em>, <em>R</em><sub>e,</sub> and <em>M,</em> are covariant with the redshift and velocity dispersion. The fundamental plane of elliptical galaxies allows us to infer <em>R</em><sub><em>e</em></sub> and <em>M</em> given <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn11.gif\" alt=\"${\\sigma }_{V}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\sigma }_{V}</script></span></span>; we use the functional form and scatter as derived in Hyde &amp; Bernardi (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib24\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib24\">2009</a>). The final parameter is the flattening, which is known to correlate with <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn12.gif\" alt=\"${\\sigma }_{V}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\sigma }_{V}</script></span></span>; more massive galaxies tend to be closer to spherical than less massive ones. This trend is clearly seen in the SDSS data, which we fit with a Rayleigh distribution, where the Rayleigh scale parameter <em>s</em> is linear in <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn13.gif\" alt=\"${\\sigma }_{V}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\sigma }_{V}</script></span></span>\n</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj519046eqn4\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046eqn4.gif\" alt=\"Equation (4)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&P(1-q| s=(A+B{\\sigma }_{V}))=\\displaystyle \\frac{1-q}{{s}^{2}}\\mathrm{exp}(\\displaystyle \\frac{-{(1-q)}^{2}}{2{s}^{2}}).\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t4\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>The fit values are <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn14.gif\" alt=\"$A=0.38$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">A=0.38</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn15.gif\" alt=\"$B=5.7\\times {10}^{-4}\\;{(\\mathrm{km}\\;{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1})}^{-1})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">B=5.7\\times {10}^{-4}\\;{(\\mathrm{km}\\;{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1})}^{-1})</script></span></span>. To exclude highly flattened mass profiles, we truncate the Rayleigh distribution at <em>q</em> = 0.2.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj519046s2-2\" name=\"apj519046s2-2\">2.2.\u00a0The Background Source Population</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>For the lensed background sources we only need to know their redshifts, light profiles, colors, and absolute magnitudes. Since typical lensed sources are at high redshift, the light profiles and spectroscopic redshifts of the unlensed source population are often beyond the capability of current telescopes. Neglecting intrinsically faint, highly magnified sources could potentially cause us to significantly underestimate the detectable strong lens population; we therefore use the sky catalogs simulated for the LSST collaboration by Connolly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib13\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib13\">2010</a>). The Connolly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib13\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib13\">2010</a>) catalog is generated by ray-tracing through the Millennium dark matter simulation (Springel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib63\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib63\">2005</a>), with galaxies added using the semi-analytic prescription of De Lucia et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib15\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib15\">2006</a>) adjusted to mimic the luminosity and color distributions of low redshift galaxies and the redshift and number-magnitude distributions derived from deep imaging and spectroscopic surveys. The final catalog comprises a 4.5 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 4.5 square degree footprint on the sky and samples halo masses down to 2.5 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>9</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. Due to the resolution limit of the simulations, this catalog is only complete down to <em>i</em> ~ 27.5, which may be insufficiently deep to perfectly reconstruct the faintest lens population of LSST but sufficient for the other surveys that we investigate here.</p><p>For all sources, we assume that the light profile is given by an elliptical exponential profile with ellipticity drawn from a Rayleigh distribution with scale parameter <em>s</em> = 0.3, truncated at <em>q</em> = 0.2. For the effective radius of the profile we assume</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj519046eqn5\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046eqn5.gif\" alt=\"Equation (5)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\mathrm{log}}_{10}(\\displaystyle \\frac{{R}_{e}}{\\mathrm{kpc}})={(\\displaystyle \\frac{{M}_{V}}{-19.5})}^{-0.22}{(\\displaystyle \\frac{1.+z}{5.})}^{-1.2}+N(0.3),\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t5\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn16.gif\" alt=\"$N(0.3)$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">N(0.3)</script></span></span> is a Gaussianly distributed random variable of scatter 0.3. The relation and scatter are in line with the results of Mosleh et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib42\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib42\">2012</a>) and Huang et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib23\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib23\">2013</a>), which both show trends toward more compact sources at higher redshifts.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj519046s2-3\" name=\"apj519046s2-3\">2.3.\u00a0The Properties of the Realistic Lens Systems, before Observing with a Telescope</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Given the lens and source populations generated in Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj519046s2-1\">2.1</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj519046s2-2\">2.2</a>, we can project the lensing cross section back onto the source population, giving the expected distribution of lens and source properties for an idealized survey. Our ideal model predicts 11 million lenses on the whole sky, with a lens velocity dispersion of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn17.gif\" alt=\"${\\sigma }_{V}\\gt 100$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\sigma }_{V}\\gt 100</script></span></span> km s<sup>\u22121</sup> and a background source with <em>i</em> &lt; 27. The Einstein radius distribution, deflector velocity dispersion distribution, and the source and lens-redshift distributions are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj519046f1\">1</a>. The model predicts that the population of strong lenses is dominated by galaxies with <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn18.gif\" alt=\"${\\sigma }_{V}\\approx 200$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\sigma }_{V}\\approx 200</script></span></span> km s<sup>\u22121</sup> and the distribution is well-approximated by a Gaussian with a mean of 210 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> and a one sigma width of 54 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>. The majority of lenses have Einstein radii around 0.5 arcsec and sources close to the completeness limit of the source catalog. Even after strong lensing magnification most sources are too faint to be detectable by Euclid, and the Einstein radii are too small to be resolvable with LSST.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj519046f1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj519046f1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046f1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046f1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0Properties of the ideal lens sample, i.e., all galaxy\u2013galaxy lenses in the universe with a deflector with <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn19.gif\" alt=\"${\\sigma }_{V}\\gt 100$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\sigma }_{V}\\gt 100</script></span></span> km s<sup>\u22121</sup> and a source with unlensed <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn20.gif\" alt=\"$i\\lt 27$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">i\\lt 27</script></span></span>. The left panel shows <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn21.gif\" alt=\"$\\frac{d}{d{\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}}(\\mathrm{log}\\;n)$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\frac{d}{d{\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}}(\\mathrm{log}\\;n)</script></span></span>. The middle panel shows the distribution of lens redshifts as the solid line and the distribution of source redshifts as the dashed line. The right panel shows the number of lenses in each <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn23.gif\" alt=\"${\\sigma }_{V}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\sigma }_{V}</script></span></span> bin of width 10 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> and the dashed line shows a Gaussian fit to this distribution, with a mean of 210 and a width of 54 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj519046f1\" href=\"/0004-637X/811/1/20/downloadFigure/figure/apj519046f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj519046f1\" href=\"/0004-637X/811/1/20/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj519046f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj519046s3\" name=\"apj519046s3\">3.\u00a0SIMULATED OBSERVATIONS OF STRONG LENSES WITH SURVEY TELESCOPES</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In practice it is impossible to observe the idealized noiseless, unconvolved lenses produced by our model; real observations of strong lenses will involve the convolution with a point-spread function and various sources of noise. To investigate the discoverable lens population in future surveys, we must therefore include the transfer function of each survey telescope. We simulate observations by CFHTLS, DES, LSST, and Euclid.</p><p>Our prescription for the transfer function is a simple one, but it encapsulates all of the significant features relevant to lens finding. For each survey, the ideal lenses are evaluated on a pixellated grid with a pixel-scale equal to the detector pixel-scale,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apj519046fn1\">1</a></sup>\n then convolved with the circularly symmetric PSF discretized to the sample pixel-scale. We then simulate the noise assuming Poisson noise from the lens, the source, and a uniform sky background, and a constant read-noise for each exposure. We neglect cosmic rays, artifacts, and ghosts. For complete and ongoing surveys, the seeing, sky-brightness, zero-points, exposure-times, number of exposures, pixel-scale, read-noise, filter set and survey area are taken from the existing observations. For future surveys we use the LSST observation simulator (Connolly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib13\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib13\">2010</a>) and the Euclid design parameters. The assumed survey parameters are summarized in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj519046t1\">1</a>.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apj519046t1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0\nProperties of Large Area Imaging Surveys Considered in This Work\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" numbers based on assumptions that we describe in the text; given are survey area \u03a9, effective collecting area, filter set, median seeing in each filter (FWHM), median sky-brightness in each filter, total exposure time per filter, and pixel-scale.</p>\n<a name=\"apj519046fn2\" id=\"apj519046fn2\"></a><sup> a</sup>We do not directly use the collecting area in our analysis; we instead use the instrumental zero-points for each survey for CFHT (Boulade et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib6\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib6\">2003</a>) and DES (Diehl et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib17\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib17\">2008</a>), for LSST we rescale the DES zero-points by the ratio of collecting areas. For Euclid we assume a zero-point of 25.5 AB (S. Niemi 2015, private communication).\n<a name=\"apj519046fn3\" id=\"apj519046fn3\"></a><sup> b</sup>Median quantities shown.\n<a name=\"apj519046fn4\" id=\"apj519046fn4\"></a><sup> c</sup>We only include these filters in our strong lensing model. We approximate <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn28.gif\" alt=\"$g,r$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">g,r</script></span></span>, and <em>i</em> filters with their SDSS counterparts.\n<a name=\"apj519046fn5\" id=\"apj519046fn5\"></a><sup> d</sup>We approximate <em>VIS</em> magnitudes as <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn29.gif\" alt=\"${VIS}=(r+i+z)/3$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{VIS}=(r+i+z)/3</script></span></span> (S. Niemi 2015, private communication).\n<a name=\"apj519046fn6\" id=\"apj519046fn6\"></a><sup> e</sup>There is some variation in sky-brightness due to zodiacal and scattered light, but we adopt 22.2 as the <em>VIS</em> sky-brightness for all Euclid pointings. We also neglect the fact that half of the Euclid area will have 2150 s total exposure time due to the dithering pattern. (S. Niemi 2015, private communication.)\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/811/1/20/suppdata/apj519046t1_ascii.txt?doi=10.1088/0004-637X/811/1/20\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/811/1/20/suppdata/apj519046t1_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>The seeing and sky-brightness are stochastic parameters that vary significantly over the course of a survey, hence for each simulated exposure we draw from the real (or expected) distribution of seeing and sky-brightness observed <em>in each filter set</em>. This point is significant to the expected number of discoverable strong lenses for two reasons. First, survey strategies are optimized to take bluer-band data in moonless skies and redder-band data when the moon is up. Second, survey strategies are often optimized for specific science goals; for example, DES preferentially takes <em>r</em>- and <em>i</em>-band data in good seeing to improve the weak lensing analysis (Diehl et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib16\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib16\">2014</a>). Survey strategies therefore lead to seeing and sky-brightness distributions that are often strongly correlated, and not random draws from the seeing and sky-brightness distributions of the telescope site. The most significant feature for lens finding is the strategy used for allocating good and bad seeing time. Since the majority of background sources are blue (Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib20\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib20\">2014</a>) and the Einstein radius distribution of our lenses peaks at ~0.5 arcsec, strategies that penalize <em>g</em>-band seeing are likely to limit the potential for discovering strong lenses.</p><p>In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj519046f2\">2</a> we show an example of a simulated lens as would be observed with each of CFHT, DES, LSST, and Euclid.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj519046f2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj519046f2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046f2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046f2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Simulated lens as would be observed by forthcoming telescopes. From left to right, the top row shows a <em>gri</em> color composite image for the full-depth co-add of CFHT, DES, and LSST. The top right shows the Euclid image. From left to right the second row shows the <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn35.gif\" alt=\"$g-\\alpha i$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">g-\\alpha i</script></span></span> difference image corresponding to the color composite above. Since Euclid has only one optical band, the second row on the far right shows the VIS residual assuming the galaxy can be subtracted perfectly (although the noise on the galaxy light remains). From left to right, the third row shows the <em>gri</em> composites for the best single-epoch DES imaging, the optimally stacked DES imaging, the best single-epoch LSST imaging, and the optimally stacked LSST imaging. The fourth row shows the <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn36.gif\" alt=\"$g-\\alpha i$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">g-\\alpha i</script></span></span> difference image corresponding to the color composite above. Our signal-to-noise detectability criteria are satisfied in all images, but we do not consider the lens to be detectable in the CFHT, DES, and LSST full co-adds as the arcs are not resolved. The CFHT full stack only marginally fails the resolution criteria, while the optimally stacked LSST image only marginally satisfies it.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj519046f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/811/1/20/downloadFigure/figure/apj519046f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj519046f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/811/1/20/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj519046f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj519046s4\" name=\"apj519046s4\">4.\u00a0DEFINING A DETECTABLE LENS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Having generated a population of strong lenses and simulated observations of them, we have ~10<sup>9</sup> exposures. While each exposure contains a simulated strong lens, not all of them can plausibly be detectable as a strong lenses. Systems with very low signal-to-noise or where the Einstein radius is much less than the seeing are unlikely to be distinguishable as strong lenses. Robotic strong lens finders already exist (e.g., Marshall et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib36\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib36\">2005b</a>), but require a large amount of survey specific tuning and a realistic training set. Most imaging-based strong lens searches require at least some level of human input. Human classifications introduce a stochastic element (e.g., Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib20\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib20\">2014</a>), but a human expert tends to look for defining features when assessing the probability of strong lensing. The patterns a human lens finder looks for are typically arc-like features, the presence of a similar-color counter image on the opposite side of the lens, features that are a different color (typically bluer) to the putative lens galaxy, and a morphology that is plausible fit by a strong lens model. Systems that meet all of these criteria are typically classified as A-grade strong lens candidates; B-grade candidates often show arc-like features without an obvious counter-image, or multiple same-color sources that are too small to show tangential shearing. The final confirmation of strong lensing typically requires either spectroscopic confirmation of the source redshift, or a compelling lens model. While spectroscopy is expensive, we optimistically assume that any system obviously featuring detectable arcs is a discoverable strong lens, given that all of our systems can be fitted with a strong lens model.</p><p>For an SIS strong lens, the centers of the counter-images are separated by twice the Einstein radius, and the magnification is purely tangential (Schneider et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib55\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib55\">1992</a>). Thus the quadrature sum of the seeing and twice the source size must be less than twice the Einstein radius, or counter-images will be unresolved. For tangential shearing to be observable the source must be resolved in the tangential direction, i.e., the product of the source size and the magnification must be greater than the seeing; additionally the magnification must be sufficiently large that the source is noticeably sheared. Based on visual inspection of lensed galaxy subtracted residuals we assume that a magnification of at least three and a signal-to-noise of at least 20 is required to unambiguously recognize arcs. By definition our strong lenses show a morphology consistent with strong lensing, but we do not insist that the source and lens be significantly different colors as color information may not be required for future lens finders (e.g., Joseph et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib28\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib28\">2014</a>).</p><p>Our criteria for a detectable strong lens are that the center of the source is multiply imaged</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj519046eqn6\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046eqn6.gif\" alt=\"Equation (6)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}^{2}\\gt {x}_{s}^{2}+{y}_{s}^{2},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t6\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn37.gif\" alt=\"${\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}</script></span></span> is the Einstein radius and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn38.gif\" alt=\"$({x}_{s},{y}_{s})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">({x}_{s},{y}_{s})</script></span></span> are the unlensed source position. The image and counter-image must be resolved</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj519046eqn7\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046eqn7.gif\" alt=\"Equation (7)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}^{2}\\gt {r}_{s}^{2}+{(s/2)}^{2},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t7\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>s</em> is the seeing and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn39.gif\" alt=\"${r}_{s},({x}_{s},{y}_{s})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{r}_{s},({x}_{s},{y}_{s})</script></span></span> is the unlensed source size. Tangential shearing of the arcs must be detectable;</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj519046eqn8\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046eqn8.gif\" alt=\"Equation (8)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\mu }_{\\mathrm{TOT}}{r}_{s}\\gt s,\\ {\\mu }_{\\mathrm{TOT}}\\gt 3,\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t8\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn40.gif\" alt=\"${\\mu }_{\\mathrm{TOT}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\mu }_{\\mathrm{TOT}}</script></span></span> is the total magnification of the source. We also require that the source be detected with sufficient signal-to-noise for a human to recognize that Equations (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apj519046eqn6\">6</a>)\u2013(<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apj519046eqn8\">8</a>) are satisfied.</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj519046eqn9\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046eqn9.gif\" alt=\"Equation (9)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\mathrm{SN}}_{\\mathrm{TOT}}\\gt 20,\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t9\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn41.gif\" alt=\"${\\mathrm{SN}}_{\\mathrm{TOT}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\mathrm{SN}}_{\\mathrm{TOT}}</script></span></span> is the total signal-to-noise of the lensed residual.</p><p>We insist that all detectable lenses obey Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apj519046eqn6\">6</a>) and that Equations (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apj519046eqn7\">7</a>) through (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apj519046eqn9\">9</a>) be satisfied in at least one imaging band. This is more constrictive than the simplest definition of a strong lens (multiple imaging of some part of the source); however, we would be surprised if future lens finders can discover a significant population of galaxy\u2013galaxy lenses that do not pass our selection cuts. The one class of galaxy\u2013galaxy lenses that will be discoverable but does not pass our criteria are systems with an unresolved source whose image morphology is still sufficient to detect lensing, i.e., quadruple image systems with sufficiently large Einstein radii and a sufficiently blue source to resolve the images from the lens galaxy; this population is likely to be small.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj519046s5\" name=\"apj519046s5\">5.\u00a0CFHTLS: TESTING THE MODEL AGAINST A WELL-STUDIED LENS SAMPLE</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The CFHTLS has been extensively searched for strong lenses, both visually (More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib41\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib41\">2015</a>) and semi-automatically (More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib40\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib40\">2012</a>; Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib20\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib20\">2014</a>), yielding approximately a hundred confirmed lenses ranging from galaxy to group scales. Our simulations generate the types of lenses that form the SL2S galaxy-scale sample of Sonnenfeld et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib59\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib59\">2013a</a>), discovered by Gavazzi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib20\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib20\">2014</a>) using the <span class=\"small-caps\">Ringfinder</span> algorithm. Applying <span class=\"small-caps\">Ringfinder</span> to the real data yielded 401 candidates, of which 49 are spectroscopically confirmed. Based on incomplete follow-up, Gavazzi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib20\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib20\">2014</a>) predicts 220 discoverable lenses in CFHT. Applying our detectability criteria to our simulated CFHTLS co-adds, we forecast 800 discoverable strong lenses. However, after applying the PSF cross-convolution and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn42.gif\" alt=\"$g-\\alpha i$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">g-\\alpha i</script></span></span> difference imaging approach of Gavazzi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib20\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib20\">2014</a>) we would only classify 290 of our candidates as still discoverable. Roughly half of the lost systems are due to the poorer seeing in the <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn43.gif\" alt=\"$g-\\alpha i$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">g-\\alpha i</script></span></span> difference image and the other half are lost due to the degraded signal-to-noise. After applying the lens galaxy cuts of Gavazzi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib20\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib20\">2014</a>), the number of detectable lenses decreases to 230 systems, in good agreement with the results of Gavazzi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib20\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib20\">2014</a>). We should not expect our forecast and the CFHT results to agree this well; <span class=\"small-caps\">Ringfinder</span> can find lenses with <em>\u03bc</em> &lt; 3 if the source is intrinsically tangential to the lens, and does not have strict criteria on total signal-to-noise or on the seeing, both potentially increasing the discoverable lens population. Conversely, some of our simulated lenses that a human would consider obvious candidates are missed by the automated part of <span class=\"small-caps\">Ringfinder</span>. This was found by Gavazzi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib20\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib20\">2014</a>) where 77 serendipitously discovered probable lenses during the development of <span class=\"small-caps\">Ringfinder</span> that were not part of the final statistical sample of 330 candidates.</p><p>In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj519046f3\">3</a> we compare the Einstein radii of the forecast 200 lenses with the values inferred by Sonnenfeld et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib59\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib59\">2013a</a>) from modeling the SL2S sample and show that the distribution of Einstein radii are similar, thus our seeing criteria (Equations (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apj519046eqn7\">7</a>) and (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apj519046eqn8\">8</a>)) are reasonably representative of what is achievable with an automatic ring-finder.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj519046f3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj519046f3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046f3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046f3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Einstein radius of the SL2S galaxy-scale lens sample, as fit by Sonnenfeld et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib59\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib59\">2013a</a>) shown in the gray bars and the Einstein radii distribution forecast by our model shown by the black dashed line, assuming a <span class=\"small-caps\">Ringfinder</span>-like search of our simulated SL2S survey. The area under the black dashed line corresponds to 230 lenses. The red bars have been scaled to have a total weight of 220 systems, equally up-weighting each of the 52 lenses modeled by Sonnenfeld et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib59\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib59\">2013a</a>) to match the predictions of Gavazzi et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib20\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib20\">2014</a>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj519046f3\" href=\"/0004-637X/811/1/20/downloadFigure/figure/apj519046f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj519046f3\" href=\"/0004-637X/811/1/20/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj519046f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Given the similarity of both the number of discoverable lenses and the Einstein radius distribution with that found in Sonnenfeld et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib59\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib59\">2013a</a>) and Gavazzi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib20\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib20\">2014</a>) we are confident that our lens population and detectability criteria are good representations of what is discoverable in the real universe. In the rest of this work we will report the number of lenses detectable in one band assuming Poisson limited galaxy subtraction (800 in the CFHTLS co-adds) as a target for future lens finders. We will also report the number of lenses discoverable in the <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn44.gif\" alt=\"$g-\\alpha i$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">g-\\alpha i</script></span></span> difference image, although we will not apply the <span class=\"small-caps\">Ringfinder</span> cuts to lens redshift and <em>i</em>-band magnitude, and assume that the PSF of the difference image can be set to the worst of the <em>g</em>- or <em>i</em>-band PSFs rather than cross-convolving the PSFs. These seem to be plausible upgrades to <span class=\"small-caps\">Ringfinder</span> and give a forecast of 480 lenses discoverable in the CFHT co-adds. This forecast represents a more pessimistic (but more achievable) view of where lens finding is likely to be in the next decade.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj519046s6\" name=\"apj519046s6\">6.\u00a0DES, LSST, AND EUCLID: ONGOING AND FUTURE LENS SURVEYS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We now consider the number of detectable lenses in the DES, LSST, and Euclid wide surveys. In the final co-added images of each survey we forecast that DES can discover 1400 lenses, LSST can discover 39000 lenses, and Euclid can discover 170000 lenses (Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj519046t2\">2</a>). Requiring that the lenses be discoverable in the <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/811/1/20/Full/apj519046ieqn45.gif\" alt=\"$g-\\alpha i$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">g-\\alpha i</script></span></span> image decreases these numbers to 800 and 16000 for DES and LSST, respectively. Since Euclid has only one visible filter it is not possible to construct an optical blue-minus-red image so no lenses can be found this way; it may be possible to use the near infrared channels, although they have decreased depth and resolution, or to use lower resolution multi-band optical data to add color information to the VIS imaging (e.g., Loncan et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj519046bib34\" id=\"fnref-apj519046bib34\">2015</a>), but we have not investigated these possibilities.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apj519046t2\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 2.</b>\u00a0\nThe Predicted Number of Discoverable Lenses Using the Model Described in This Work Applied to Current and Future Large Area Surveys\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj519046t2\" data-toolbar-img=\"/0004-637X/811/1/20/suppdata/apj519046t2_lr.gif\"
